  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_159d90b3-a1d2-4e7b-bdc2-4a20035ed749'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer3350570558173032528">In the year 1754, the stillness of Charlestown, New Hampshire, is shattered by the terrifying cries of an Indian raid. Young Miriam Willard, on a day that had promised new happiness, finds herself instead a captive on a forest trail, caught up in the ebb and flow of the French and Indian War.<br/> It is a harrowing march north. Miriam can only force herself to the next stopping</span>
  <span id="freeText3350570558173032528" style="display:none">In the year 1754, the stillness of Charlestown, New Hampshire, is shattered by the terrifying cries of an Indian raid. Young Miriam Willard, on a day that had promised new happiness, finds herself instead a captive on a forest trail, caught up in the ebb and flow of the French and Indian War.<br /> It is a harrowing march north. Miriam can only force herself to the next stopping place, the next small portion of food, the next icy stream to be crossed. At the end of the trail waits a life of hard work and, perhaps, even a life of slavery. Mingled with her thoughts of Phineas Whitney, her sweetheart on his way to Harvard, is the crying of her sister’s baby, Captive, born on the trail.<br /> Miriam and her companions finally reach Montreal, a city of shifting loyalties filled with the intrigue of war, and here, by a sudden twist of fortune, Miriam meets the prominent Du Quesne family, who introduce her to a life she has never imagined. Based on an actual narrative diary published in 1807, Calico Captive skillfully reenacts an absorbing facet of history.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3350570558173032528" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/30858172-juliathebookworm"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1552248629i/30858172._UX100_CR0,16,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/30858172-juliathebookworm">Juliathebookworm</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Both? I found it enjoyable, but also thought that it seemed geared toward teens or preteens. Perhaps it is not as relatable for an adult. The conflict<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Both? I found it enjoyable, but also thought that it seemed geared toward teens or preteens. Perhaps it is not as relatable for an adult. The conflicts the main character goes through with in herself and some of her actions were more childish, that's why I would deem it a children's book. However, I had trouble putting it down and was very drawn into the story. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1306100378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1306100378" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1306100378?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=library-has">library-has</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=dated-social-attitudes">dated-social-attitudes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1306100378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2055533661034727363">Elizabeth George Speare is best known as the author of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703292.The_Witch_of_Blackbird_Pond' title='The Witch of Blackbird Pond by Elizabeth George Speare' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Witch of Blackbird Pond</a>, winner of the 1959 Newbury medal and a long-time favorite comfort read of mine, but I've never read any of her other books. But a few weeks ago one of my friends, Carol Storm, strongly recommended <i>Calico Captive</i> in her <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1305267177?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>GR review</a>, so I decided to grab it at the library and give it a read. <br/><br/><i>Calico Captive</i> is a fictionalized version of a few years in the life of Miriam Willard, a teenage girl who is kidnapped from he</span>
  <span id="freeText2055533661034727363" style="display:none">Elizabeth George Speare is best known as the author of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703292.The_Witch_of_Blackbird_Pond" title="The Witch of Blackbird Pond by Elizabeth George Speare" rel="nofollow noopener">The Witch of Blackbird Pond</a>, winner of the 1959 Newbury medal and a long-time favorite comfort read of mine, but I've never read any of her other books. But a few weeks ago one of my friends, Carol Storm, strongly recommended <i>Calico Captive</i> in her <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1305267177?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">GR review</a>, so I decided to grab it at the library and give it a read. <br /><br /><i>Calico Captive</i> is a fictionalized version of a few years in the life of Miriam Willard, a teenage girl who is kidnapped from her home in Charleston, New Hampshire by the Abenaki Indians in 1754, along with her very pregnant sister Susanna Johnson, Susanna's husband James, and their three young children, two-year-old Polly, four-year-old Susanna, and six-year-old Sylvanus. The Abenakis take them on a harrowing march northward. Susanna gives birth on the march and, in what I can only assume is a fit of despair, names her baby daughter "Captive." They end up staying several months in the Indians' village and are semi-adopted into the tribe. Little Sylvanus, especially, takes to the Abenaki lifestyle like a duck to water, enthusiastically learning to hunt and speak their language.<br /><br />However, when Miriam rejects the romantic overtures of a chief's son, the Indians sell most of the group--except Sylvanus, Susanna and baby Captive--to the French in Quebec as servants/slaves. Miriam's nieces are "adopted" by different French families, and Miriam tries to make a new life for herself as a servant<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, then as a guest of a wealthy family, then as a seamstress <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. She befriends a couple of the wealthier young people in Quebec, and struggles with the tensions between her old beliefs and lifestyle and the opulent life lived by many of the French Canadian people she meets.<br /><br />This was an interesting middle grade historical tale, made more interesting by the fact that it follows actual history quite closely for most of the tale. This novel harks back to the once-popular "captivity narrative" literature, memoirs by people who were kidnapped by native Americans back in frontier days. The real-life Susanna Johnson, Miriam's sister, wrote a book about her adventures entitled <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20432068.A_Narrative_of_The_Captivity_of_Mrs__Johnson__Containing_An_Account_of_Her_Sufferings__During_Four_Years__With_the_Indians_and_French" title="A Narrative of The Captivity of Mrs. Johnson; Containing An Account of Her Sufferings, During Four Years, With the Indians and French by Susanna Willard Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">A Narrative of The Captivity of Mrs. Johnson; Containing An Account of Her Sufferings, During Four Years, With the Indians and French</a>, available to read online <a href="http://eco.canadiana.ca/view/oocihm.35424/3?r=0&amp;s=1" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. The novel follows Miriam's story rather than Susanna's but, other than the Quebec portion of the story, is based largely on Susanna's narrative. Baby Captive was actually named "Elizabeth Captive," but Susanna often calls her Captive in her story. Interestingly, the real life Miriam was only 14 years old when she was kidnapped. The novel doesn't specify her age, but she reads a little bit older to me, maybe 15 or 16, given her romantic interests.<br /><br />As you might expect from a story written in the 1950s, there are a lot of cringe-worthy descriptions of the native Americans, although I'm sure it's realistic of the way people thought in the 1700s. The words "savages" and "redskins" are used a few times. The prejudices of the white people against those of other races, countries and religions are a major part of the story. To some extent Miriam's prejudices are overcome in the story as she gets to know the people who are different from her, but don't expect a complete 180 turn.<br /><br />The ending of the story was a bit abrupt for me<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[; I really wanted to read more about what happened when Miriam again met the young man who had been waiting for her <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. But in fairness, that ending is also true to Susanna's narrative. <br /><br />3.5 stars. It's somewhat dated and may offend some readers, but it's a fascinating tale with strong roots in a part of actual history that we don't hear about too often nowadays.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2055533661034727363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_116185324" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie"><img alt="Lars Guthrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199816668p2/757602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116185324?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lars Guthrie" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie">Lars Guthrie</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116185324" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13267284049684508432">There's a good reason why 'Sign of the Beaver' and 'The Witch of Blackbird Pond' are so frequently assigned (over-assigned) in elementary school. Historical fiction is a staple of language arts classes. Elizabeth George Speare is at the top of the field. Besides teachers finding merit in the two books, kids go for them.<br/><br/>Which makes it all the more shameful that Speare's two other novels (in print, anyway) are virtually ignored. <br/><br/>I read 'The Bronze Bow' a few years ago, and thought it was unbelieva</span>
  <span id="freeText13267284049684508432" style="display:none">There's a good reason why 'Sign of the Beaver' and 'The Witch of Blackbird Pond' are so frequently assigned (over-assigned) in elementary school. Historical fiction is a staple of language arts classes. Elizabeth George Speare is at the top of the field. Besides teachers finding merit in the two books, kids go for them.<br /><br />Which makes it all the more shameful that Speare's two other novels (in print, anyway) are virtually ignored. <br /><br />I read 'The Bronze Bow' a few years ago, and thought it was unbelievably great. I mean, it did win the Newbery (and more recently than 'Witch'). Yet I haven't met anyone who's read it except me. Because it's got Jesus (mostly peripherally) in it? <br /><br />Now I can report that 'Calico Captive' is just as noteworthy, notwithstanding the lack of a Newbery. A meticulous researcher who captures period colors, sounds, even smells, George never fails to present vibrant characters dealing with issues not unlike those young people confront today, as well as deliver exciting stories filled with action.<br /><br />For 'Captive,' George takes off from a first-person real-life account of four years spent as a hostage and a slave in the time of the French and American War. In the 'Narrative of the Captivity of Mrs. Johnson,' Susanna Johnson tells of her family's kidnapping by Abenaki Indians in New Hampshire, their forced march north, their sale to residents of Montreal, and their eventual return to New England in a prisoner exchange. <br /><br />George makes Susanna's younger sister, Miriam Willard, the protagonist of her tale. More headstrong and impetuous than her practical sister, Miriam prejudges Indians and French, then finds herself questioning those prejudices. Because of her talents as a dress designer, she mingles with Montreal's elite, and struggles to match her Puritan upbringing with a society invested in 'popery' and conquering English colonists. And she searches for true love. <br /><br />Highly recommended.</span>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1143547544" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1143547544" />
    <a title="Deborah Markus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus"><img alt="Deborah Markus" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1143547544?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Markus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Markus" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus">Deborah Markus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780395071120" href="/book/show/1206172.Calico_Captive">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/954028-deborah-markus?shelf=give-me-cake">give-me-cake</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1143547544" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1012524286190782833">Welcome to another episode of Deborah's Library Book Is Overdue! Today's special guest is a YA novel by Elizabeth George Speare, author of the modern classic <i>The Witch of Blackbird Pond</i>. Although Deborah read this book in a couple of hours <i>and</i> her library now allows her to renew books twice provided no one has placed a hold on the title, she still managed to put off reviewing it long enough to rack up some minor but humiliating fees. <br/><br/>Deborah also deserves some public mocking for the fact that, w</span>
  <span id="freeText1012524286190782833" style="display:none">Welcome to another episode of Deborah's Library Book Is Overdue! Today's special guest is a YA novel by Elizabeth George Speare, author of the modern classic <i>The Witch of Blackbird Pond</i>. Although Deborah read this book in a couple of hours <i>and</i> her library now allows her to renew books twice provided no one has placed a hold on the title, she still managed to put off reviewing it long enough to rack up some minor but humiliating fees. <br /><br />Deborah also deserves some public mocking for the fact that, when she noticed the book was due on December 26, she immediately concocted <b>and believed</b> a charming fantasy of lolling around with a cup of eggnog while she typed up a review on the day after Christmas, because of course she'd have nothing better to do that day. Please laugh hard enough at her that she can hear you without even opening her window! <br /><br />Thanks, folks! And now: the book!<br /><br /><i>Calico Captive</i> is a YA novel based on the story told in the 1754 memoir <i>Narrative of the Captivity of Mrs. Johnson.</i> I was lucky enough to find a copy of that narrative, and so was able to see how much truth had slipped into the novel. <br /><br />Quite a bit, as it happens. This review will focus on pointing out the nonfiction that can be found in the fiction. I hope this will be fun for people who've already read the book and, like me, always wondered how much of it was factually accurate. <br /><br />For readers unfamiliar with the novel: it's a well written historical YA novel that contains a minimum of mushy romance, though there's just enough love-interest to keep things interesting. If you liked <i>Witch of Blackbird Pond,</i> you'll enjoy this, though the main character's loathing of Native Americans is cringe-inducing at times. (It's historically accurate but pretty sad that this character doesn't change her mind any even when a white guy tries to explain some basic "they <i>were</i> here first" morality.)<br /><br />Speare wisely chooses to make Mrs. Johnson's younger sister Miriam Willard her protagonist. Miriam isn't described in too much detail in the memoir, so Speare has some room to play. Also, Susanna Johnson is an intelligent, deeply pious, extremely narrow-minded New England matron whose abhorrence and distrust of Native Americans is second only to her loathing of the French. (It's a close race, by the way. She makes it clear in her memoir that she doesn't consider either group to be fully human.) Young readers wouldn't find much to relate to in this upright Puritan lady.<br /><br />But Miriam, while sharing most of her sister's sentiments, is young – only fourteen years old when the story begins – and therefore more sympathetic and interesting to teenage readers. She's in the middle of her very first crush when Abenakis raid her home in Charlestown, New Hampshire. She and Susanna are taken prisoner, along with Susanna's husband and children. They are brought to an Abenaki village the whites called St. Francis, and eventually sent to Canada to wait to be ransomed or exchanged for French prisoners.<br /><br />All of this really happened. And in both novel and memoir, Susanna is heavily pregnant when their captivity begins. However, there was no debate among their captors as to whether the prisoners would be murdered or taken back with the Abenakis as valuable hostages. Speare invented this for dramatic tension, although it's a fairly accurate representation of the idea whites held that Indians were unpredictably violent "savages."<br /><br />Susanna does indeed lose her shoe as the family is hurried along, and the captives worry that her inability to travel very quickly will endanger all their lives. Speare sticks close to her source material all through Miriam's journey with her family to St. Francis. One detail I found interesting was that in the novel, Susanna gives birth to a baby during this forced march and names her Captive. In real life, the baby was named Elizabeth Captive. <br /><br />Both the memoir and the novel mention that the baby nearly drowns while the captives ford a stream. In the novel, Miriam is a crucial participant in a dramatic rescue; in the memoir, it's a male neighbor who'd been taken captive in the same raid who saves the baby's life.<br /><br />Rather to my surprise, the young Native American man who teases Miriam on their journey is also straight from the memoir. He feels like a character invented solely for a potential love-triangle, and in fact Speare offers a bit of romantic drama from him that's crucial to the plot but entirely fictional. But there really was such a teenaged Abenaki, described by Susanna as "a youth of sixteen, who in our journey discovered [revealed] a very mischievous and troublesome disposition." She adds that "he often delighted himself by tormenting my sister [Miriam], by pulling her hair, treading on her gown, and numerous other boyish pranks." Times change; people don't.<br /><br />Certainly teenagers don't. In the novel, Miriam doesn't see her sister Susanna's patience and strength; she only feels a bitter frustration that Susanna keeps slowing them down, first when she's heavily pregnant and then as she struggles to recover from giving birth. Apparently, this is an accurate representation of Miriam's feelings, as this line from Susanna's memoir makes clear: "My sister observed, that, if I could have been left behind, our trouble would have been seemingly nothing." <br /><br />Once the captives reach St. Francis and then white Canada, Speare drifts from her source material in order to introduce the obligatory love triangle and a rags-to-riches-to-rags-to-freedom story based entirely on a single sentence from the captivity narrative: "[Miriam] had supported herself by her needle in the family of the lieutenant governor, where she was treated extremely well."<br /><br />Susanna Johnson's captivity narrative is available online fer free; so if you read <i>Calico Captive,</i> you can then read the real story for yourself right here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://books.google.com/books/about/A_narrative_of_the_captivity_of_Mrs_John.html?id=HBpMAAAAYAAJ">http://books.google.com/books/about/A...</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="1012524286190782833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1728462827" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1728462827" />
    <a title="Sarah Brazytis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36717925-sarah-brazytis"><img alt="Sarah Brazytis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442988549p2/36717925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1728462827?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah Brazytis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah Brazytis" href="/user/show/36717925-sarah-brazytis">Sarah Brazytis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36717925-sarah-brazytis?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1728462827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3381598501807822113">The first time I read this book, I liked it fairly well. My only problem with it was the fact that the climax of the book seemed to be the moment when Miriam <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ by dancing the minuet in calico  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> shows that she understands that clothing and position do not dictate a person's worth and dignity. But the author is laboring under the delusion that the ending is the climax...but you've almost forgotten that the subject of this climax even exists! Very confusing.<br/><br/>But as a his</span>
  <span id="freeText3381598501807822113" style="display:none">The first time I read this book, I liked it fairly well. My only problem with it was the fact that the climax of the book seemed to be the moment when Miriam <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ by dancing the minuet in calico  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> shows that she understands that clothing and position do not dictate a person's worth and dignity. But the author is laboring under the delusion that the ending is the climax...but you've almost forgotten that the subject of this climax even exists! Very confusing.<br /><br />But as a historian, what knocked a solid three stars off this rating was the historical content. And I was not aware of this until I became curious enough to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1224063.A_Narrative_Of_The_Captivity_Of_Mrs__Johnson" title="A Narrative Of The Captivity Of Mrs. Johnson by Susannah Willard Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">A Narrative Of The Captivity Of Mrs. Johnson</a>, which this book was based on. <br /><br />Elizabeth George Speare did a great disservice to the Johnson family. I will try to touch a few main points below:<br /><br /><i>Susanna Johnson is portrayed as a suspicious, hard-nosed, puritanical woman. She despises Indians, sees no good in them, and scorns the aid of a kind-hearted French priest because he is Catholic.</i><br />Susanna, in her own account, was an open-hearted lady who noticed how the Indians provided for their captives' needs even before their own; appreciated the kindness of monks and priests; and even had her baby baptized in a Catholic church because the baby's illness worried their French hosts - even though she did not believe in infant baptism, she allowed the ceremony to be performed to soothe their Catholic friends' concerns. Is this a nasty, close-minded woman? She even teaches her husband card games to amuse themselves in prison! Regardless of personal beliefs regarding such things, it shows she understood the difference between rigid rules and the reason behind them.<br /><br /><i>Young Sylvanus is portrayed as a boy who quickly and easily is detached from his family, to the point of despising the weakness of his half-starved father (who, by the way, still manages to carry his ailing wife on his back for many days), ignoring his mother, and admiring their captors.</i><br />In the true account, Sylvanus is a loyal little lad who fears for his dear mother's safety, remembers her long after the memory of all his other family members have faded in years of captivity, and is only torn from them at great length and in great grief.<br /><br /><i>Miriam is desired, courted and pursued by an Indian, a Frenchman and an Englishman. What a great record!</i><br />Reality is that Miriam is detained late the night before the capture by what Susannah calls 'A spruce young spark' who wanted to 'keep company' with her a little longer, is teased by a bratty Indian boy in a grade-school style during their captivity, and no Frenchman ever appears.<br /><br /><i>Miriam saves her infant niece's life in a rushing river, thereby reattaching her to her sister and earning respect from her Indian captors for her bravery.</i><br />The true story? The neighbor man who was captured with them, Labarree, saves the baby's life. And the bond between him and this little girl he saved remains throughout his lifetime, until he is at last comforted on his deathbed by the loving young woman she has grown up to be. Their friendship brought tears to my eyes. Isn't that beautiful food for fiction?<br /><br />All in all, I am troubled and disappointed by these untruths - and they are, at this point, untruths because Elizabeth Speare knew the truth. She should have told it and honored the Johnson family. It would still have been a great story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3381598501807822113" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer343183348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10956198694633653458">Calico Captive is the fictionalized version of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1224063.A_Narrative_Of_The_Captivity_Of_Mrs__Johnson' title='A Narrative Of The Captivity Of Mrs. Johnson by Susannah Willard Johnson' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Narrative Of The Captivity Of Mrs. Johnson</a> told from the perspective of Susannah Johnson’s younger (by ten years) sister, Miriam. As would be expected with any captivity story, it’s fascinating, fast-paced and hard to put down. The author does an excellent job of portraying a young woman confronting the competing dangers of trying to stay alive, keep family together and maintain cherishes values amidst the perplexities of multi-cultures, (English,</span>
  <span id="freeText10956198694633653458" style="display:none">Calico Captive is the fictionalized version of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1224063.A_Narrative_Of_The_Captivity_Of_Mrs__Johnson" title="A Narrative Of The Captivity Of Mrs. Johnson by Susannah Willard Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">A Narrative Of The Captivity Of Mrs. Johnson</a> told from the perspective of Susannah Johnson’s younger (by ten years) sister, Miriam. As would be expected with any captivity story, it’s fascinating, fast-paced and hard to put down. The author does an excellent job of portraying a young woman confronting the competing dangers of trying to stay alive, keep family together and maintain cherishes values amidst the perplexities of multi-cultures, (English, French, Indian, and colonial) divergent religious beliefs against the backdrop of constantly changing fortunes. <br /><br />Highly recommended for young—and slightly older—readers!</span>

    <a title="Carol Storm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5032322-carol-storm"><img alt="Carol Storm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442507712p2/5032322.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1305267177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5516869838945486126">This Fifties classic is one of the greatest YA novels ever written -- in some ways it's even better than THE WITCH OF BLACKBIRD POND!<br/><br/>Elizabeth George Speare had a very special gift. She was able to create teen heroines who were strong, self-reliant, and independent -- but always with a soft spot for a cute boy. She was able to use all of the danger and intrigue of the past -- witch trials in Puritan New England, terrifying Indian raids on the frontier -- as a backdrop for the heroine's romantic </span>
  <span id="freeText5516869838945486126" style="display:none">This Fifties classic is one of the greatest YA novels ever written -- in some ways it's even better than THE WITCH OF BLACKBIRD POND!<br /><br />Elizabeth George Speare had a very special gift. She was able to create teen heroines who were strong, self-reliant, and independent -- but always with a soft spot for a cute boy. She was able to use all of the danger and intrigue of the past -- witch trials in Puritan New England, terrifying Indian raids on the frontier -- as a backdrop for the heroine's romantic adventures, in a way that made every page seem to turn by itself! More than that, there was always a sort of hard to define sweetness to her stories, an innocence . . . as if you always knew somehow that the heroine would triumph in the end, that there was goodness in even the darkest places. That plus the fact that she could make a girl gulping down a mug of hot chocolate under the knowing eyes of a handsome young French adventurer seem steamier than the hottest sex! <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

  <span id="freeText7369161474944264184" style="display:none">Cleanliness:<br /><br />Sexual Content<br />Lust - 4 Incidents: “A man looks boldly at a girl in a way that was … disturbing.” Some girls tease about liking and marrying certain guys. A girl is envious of another girl having a dance partner to take her to the ball. A girl notices how a young man looks at her and makes her uncomfortable.<br /><br />Sexual Miscellaneous - 14 Incidents: There are a few dances in the book. A girl undresses for the night. “Two of the savages… dragging a shrinking and almost naked Susanna.” At night “Miriam was forced to lie down between two Indians, a heavy cord thrown over her body and held securely under theirs.” A woman gives birth (not detailed). Mentions often that Indians are half naked (shirtless). “If they expect me to undress while they all stand and watch me and laugh at me, they can wait all night.” “I marry Pierre, have French babies.” A girl’s clothes were ripped, exposing her shoulder. Two ladies wear dresses that are “scandalous low.” A young man compliments a girl and touches her curls. A girl spends time with a young man and he touches her elbow and holds her hand while walking. That night she thinks about him and another guy she likes. A girl receives a letter from a boy she likes and he makes it understood that he really likes her. The girl thinks on this. A young man proposes to and kisses a girl.<br /><br />Profanity<br />Mild Obscenities &amp; Substitutions - 1 Incident: drat<br /><br />Scatological Terms - 1 Incident: Bl**dy (as in lots of blood)<br /><br />Religious Profanities - 9 Incidents: when heaven only knew, God forgive me, praise be, goodness no, thank goodness, good heavens, by all the saints, oh God in heaven<br /><br />Derogatory Terms - 1 Incident: old hag<br /><br />Conversation Topics - 4 Incidents: A pipe is smoked. Catholicism is discussed. Although a girl is not catholic, she wonders if it is so wrong. Card games and dice are mentioned. Wine is mentioned and a character gets drunk.<br /><br />**Like my reviews? Then you should follow me! Because I have hundreds more just like this one. With each review, I provide a Cleanliness Report, mentioning any objectionable content I come across so that parents and/or conscientious readers (like me) can determine beforehand whether they want to read a book or not. Content surprises are super annoying, especially when you’re 100+ pages in, so here’s my attempt to help you avoid that! <br /><br />So Follow or Friend me here on GoodReads! You’ll see my updates as I’m reading and know which books I’m liking and what I’m not finishing and why. You’ll also be able to utilize my library for looking up titles to see whether the book you’re thinking about reading next has any objectionable content or not. From swear words, to romance, to bad attitudes (in children’s books), I cover it all!<br /></span>

<div id="review_2197938034" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2197938034" />
    <a title="Laura (Book Scrounger)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33792597-laura-book-scrounger"><img alt="Laura (Book Scrounger)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541274882p2/33792597.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura (Book Scrounger)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura (Book Scrounger)" href="/user/show/33792597-laura-book-scrounger">Laura (Book Scrounger)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33792597-laura-book-scrounger?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33792597-laura-book-scrounger?shelf=4th-to-middle-grade">4th-to-middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33792597-laura-book-scrounger?shelf=1750-1799">1750-1799</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2197938034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14615815114998775501">Elizabeth George Speare was one of my favorite authors during my adolescent years. I fell in love with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24408.The_Bronze_Bow' title='The Bronze Bow by Elizabeth George Speare' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Bronze Bow</a>, and really enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703292.The_Witch_of_Blackbird_Pond' title='The Witch of Blackbird Pond by Elizabeth George Speare' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Witch of Blackbird Pond</a>. I loved how Speare incorporated romance into her stories in a very subtle way, and how her endings always seemed to finish on just the right note.<br/><br/>Somehow I never got around to reading this one, until a few months ago when I found it at a used book sale. <br/><br/>Overall I really enjoyed it -- unlike her other works of historical fiction, th</span>
  <span id="freeText14615815114998775501" style="display:none">Elizabeth George Speare was one of my favorite authors during my adolescent years. I fell in love with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24408.The_Bronze_Bow" title="The Bronze Bow by Elizabeth George Speare" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bronze Bow</a>, and really enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703292.The_Witch_of_Blackbird_Pond" title="The Witch of Blackbird Pond by Elizabeth George Speare" rel="nofollow noopener">The Witch of Blackbird Pond</a>. I loved how Speare incorporated romance into her stories in a very subtle way, and how her endings always seemed to finish on just the right note.<br /><br />Somehow I never got around to reading this one, until a few months ago when I found it at a used book sale. <br /><br />Overall I really enjoyed it -- unlike her other works of historical fiction, this one is actually based on true events. It's the story of a family who is captured by Abenaki Indians in the mid-1700s and taken to Montreal where they are sold to the French. This story focuses not on Susanna Johnson who eventually wrote the narrative of these events, but on her teenage sister Miriam. <br /><br />Part of me wishes I'd read this as a teenager, because I would probably have identified more with Miriam -- not that I don't now, but I also identified with her older sister who was separated from most of her young children, which scares me far more than the other events of this story.<br /><br />I liked the way that Miriam eventually takes responsibility and works to better her situation. In the beginning it was really hard to understand what was going on in her head -- being captured by Indians but worrying more about how dirty her dress was getting than her sister's children? (Hence why I identify more with Susanna.)<br /><br />I liked the "love triangle" as well, especially the way it ends up. It's similar to the endings of Speare's other books, where there is just enough of a hint to know what is going to happen, but the story ends before it quite gets to be completely resolved -- it's a great spark for the imagination.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14615815114998775501" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_80489210" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80489210" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annette" href="/user/show/929750-annette">Annette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone!</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/929750-annette?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/929750-annette?shelf=5-star-fictional-favorites">5-star-fictional-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer80489210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16961947217856079328">This is the beautifully written first novel of Elizabeth George Speare. Although I enjoyed all of her other books, this one is my favorite. I am completely baffled as to why this is the only book by Ms. Speare that did not win a Newbery award. <br/><br/>I love all of the characters! I love the high-spirited, red-haired main character, Miriam Willard, as well as her delightful, and good natured, best friend, Hortense. I couldn't help but admire her sister, Susanna's quiet, steady strength as she endured on</span>
  <span id="freeText16961947217856079328" style="display:none">This is the beautifully written first novel of Elizabeth George Speare. Although I enjoyed all of her other books, this one is my favorite. I am completely baffled as to why this is the only book by Ms. Speare that did not win a Newbery award. <br /><br />I love all of the characters! I love the high-spirited, red-haired main character, Miriam Willard, as well as her delightful, and good natured, best friend, Hortense. I couldn't help but admire her sister, Susanna's quiet, steady strength as she endured one heartwrenching trial after another. I laughed out loud at the tall and handsome, Pierre's quick and clever wit.<br /><br />Ms. Speare is such a good writer, I was entranced from the very beginning all the way to the very end. I really enjoyed watching Miriam go from being a self-absorbed teenager to a self-confident young woman. Through her many harrowing experiences she learns important life lessons about love and loyalty. <br /><br />I'm sure that one of the reasons I enjoyed this book so much is because C.M. Herbert did such an excellent job of reading it. I listened to the Blackstone Audiobook version on CD and Ms. Herbert did a beautiful job, especially on the characters with French accents. I couldn't wait to put the next CD in so I could find out what would happen next.<br /><br />So basically what I'm saying is, Calico Captive is very well written and well worth the read. I highly recommend it!<br /> </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer196559302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15180698053822845507">This was just as good as I remembered! There is just the right mix of adventure, suspense, romance, friendship, and character growth . . . and ball gowns and hot chocolate. Plus, there are plenty of twists and turns to keep things interesting. I think this would make a great movie, if done right. My favorite parts of the book are once they get to Montreal (thankfully, that's the majority of the book). I just love how the city at this period is brought to life. Elizabeth George Speare is a master</span>
  <span id="freeText15180698053822845507" style="display:none">This was just as good as I remembered! There is just the right mix of adventure, suspense, romance, friendship, and character growth . . . and ball gowns and hot chocolate. Plus, there are plenty of twists and turns to keep things interesting. I think this would make a great movie, if done right. My favorite parts of the book are once they get to Montreal (thankfully, that's the majority of the book). I just love how the city at this period is brought to life. Elizabeth George Speare is a master at bringing history alive, and this book is no exception. It made me want to read more books that take place in New France and/or during the French and Indian War. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[It was really sad how the family was divided up. I'm so glad that they were finally all reunited; it was kind of stressing me out that they might not be! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Highly recommended.<br /><br />P.S. I like the fact that this has illustrations. Maybe it's just me, but young adult and adult books don’t seem to have illustrations as much as they used to.<br /><br /></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22106081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17417597759923884911"><i>Calico Captive</i> is one of my favorite books from childhood; I picked it up in the library when I was 9 or 10 and consistently returned to it over time.<br/><br/>The story is set during the French and Indian Wars before the American Revolution; the main character, Miriam, lives with her sister, Susannah and her husband and several small children. They are taken captive by Indians, then eventually traded to the French to live in Montreal, where they serve as domestic staff to a wealthy family.<br/><br/>Miriam's story </span>
  <span id="freeText17417597759923884911" style="display:none"><i>Calico Captive</i> is one of my favorite books from childhood; I picked it up in the library when I was 9 or 10 and consistently returned to it over time.<br /><br />The story is set during the French and Indian Wars before the American Revolution; the main character, Miriam, lives with her sister, Susannah and her husband and several small children. They are taken captive by Indians, then eventually traded to the French to live in Montreal, where they serve as domestic staff to a wealthy family.<br /><br />Miriam's story captures these events through the eyes of a teenager, and her sometimes quite self-centered reactions to what was happening. My favorite parts of the books were the descriptions of the French people who lived in Montreal at the time, and their balls and lifestyle. <br /><br />This book is very different than the rough-hewn pioneer accounts that characterize most early American stories, and fun because it gives a face to a period of American history that you don't hear a whole lot about.</span>

<div id="review_2525471395" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2525471395" />
    <a title="Kathryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78256454-kathryn"><img alt="Kathryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519655920p2/78256454.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2525471395?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn" href="/user/show/78256454-kathryn">Kathryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2525471395&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2525471395" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9884802415207688833">I really enjoyed this book. I read it quickly, which I normally don’t do, which means it peaked my interest. I found myself very emotionally invested in the characters and found myself empathizing with their situation to the point where I would feel sad when they were sad and happy when they were happy.<br/><br/>This book follows the main character of teenager Miriam Willard, her sister, brother-in-law, and their three children during the beginning phases and conclusion of the French and Indian War. Miria</span>
  <span id="freeText9884802415207688833" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book. I read it quickly, which I normally don’t do, which means it peaked my interest. I found myself very emotionally invested in the characters and found myself empathizing with their situation to the point where I would feel sad when they were sad and happy when they were happy.<br /><br />This book follows the main character of teenager Miriam Willard, her sister, brother-in-law, and their three children during the beginning phases and conclusion of the French and Indian War. Miriam Willard is in love and living her day to day life, when one night everything is turned on it’s head. Miriam and her family are captured by Indians and taken on a long and perilous journey through the wilderness to be sold to French soldiers in Montreal. They all individually have their own set of trials, when they are separated and sold to different buyers. Do not fret, this story has a happy ending BUT... here are my thoughts:<br /><br />The overall story is emotionally and mentally complex. Why you may ask? The reasoning is that it keeps you thinking. In particular, the scenario of who and why she ends up with the person she chooses. My sister who read this book agrees with my statement of “this book is frustrating but good at the same time.” <br /><br /> I like who she ends up with but I don’t think this is truly who she was meant to be with. I feel this story had a rushed ending. Miriam’s character development happened too quickly. Miriam starts out as this Puritan girl who tries to mask her materialistic desires by trying to do what is right. She loves fashion and high society but when her sister Susanna (down to earth, kind, humble, and simple) sort of gets her to see the bigger picture about life and what is truly important, Miriam begins to slowly change, develop, and mature. The key word is “slowly.” <br /><br />Well in the ending chapters, Miriam, in my opinion, out of the blue changes and becomes more mature and thinks things through. She suddenly puts aside her materialistic desires and becomes level headed, when all of her dreams and desires are going to be handed to her on a silver platter. Bravo to this, but is it realistic considering how impulsive and materialistic she is? Her development and maturity happened too quickly to me. I feel more details and story should have been added to build up to this big change of Miriam’s character.<br /><br />Miriam had pretty much forgotten about Phineas. She couldn’t remember his voice and his face after all of this time, which is realistic. When he sends her the letter, she doesn’t know how she feels, understandable. She talks about how she has changed and is no longer that girl from back then. But then a few chapters later, all of a sudden memories flood back and she loves him. I think it's supposed to be like she matured and realizes that she doesn’t trust Pierre. It’s just very wishy washy and she does a complete 180 from who she truly is. The time frame of this change just doesn’t add up to me. Like I said, more needed to be added to build up to this change of character. <br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed this book. I feel that who Miriam ends up with was meant for the readers (also for the historical accuracy to the account it is based off of.) I feel that, based on the way Miriam’s character was written, and what I truly believe is Miriam’s true underlying character, that she would have chosen Pierre over Phineas. I feel Miriam’s character development happened too quickly and that’s what throws me off with this book. I feel that if the ending wasn’t so rushed, and there were more details and add ins, this book would have felt more understanding, complete and satisfying. Details and build up would have made the ending with her and Phineas more understandable. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9884802415207688833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1509392333" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Lizzie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9374755-lizzie"><img alt="Lizzie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520183257p2/9374755.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lizzie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lizzie" href="/user/show/9374755-lizzie">Lizzie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1509392333" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15988403042195599217">Actual rating: 3.5/5 stars<br/><br/>I was thrilled to be reading this book, having recently read and adored Elizabeth’s other novel, ‘The Witch of Blackbird Pond’. I didn’t like the story in this novel as much as I did in the other one, although I still enjoyed it and rated it 3.5/5 stars.   <span id="freeText15988403042195599217" style="display:none">Actual rating: 3.5/5 stars<br /><br />I was thrilled to be reading this book, having recently read and adored Elizabeth’s other novel, ‘The Witch of Blackbird Pond’. I didn’t like the story in this novel as much as I did in the other one, although I still enjoyed it and rated it 3.5/5 stars. The writing was wonderful, it was well paced, I just didn’t find the story all that interesting.<br />	This novel featured many wonderful characters and messages, such as faith, hope, and sacrifice.<br />	I loved our main character, Miriam Willard. She wasn’t fragile as glass, but she wasn’t unbreakable either, and she didn’t pretend to be. She always believed that things would turn out alright, and I loved seeing her character develop throughout the course of this novel. Here’s a quote from the novel that I thought represented her character development well:<br /><br />	“Miriam felt astonished at herself. It was a new thing for her to step out so independently. Somehow, in the past month a tough little root of determination had been growing in her. Whether it was strong enough to support the desperate plan she had undertaken she would soon find out.”<br /><br />	Despite the many trials that came along, Miriam and her family kept their chins up, and held firm to faith. Being captured and sold by Indians, they had faith. Being brought to a strange new country, to work as slaves, they had faith. Throughout the war, they had faith. Even being stuck in jail for weeks, they had faith.<br />	They knew God would bring them through this- they knew He hadn’t forgotten them and that He was still taking care of them. And they were right to, because He brought them through it, even stronger than before. What an inspiration, right??</span>
  <a data-text-id="15988403042195599217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Melinda Ross" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1318303-melinda-ross"><img alt="Melinda Ross" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421702890p2/1318303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56039977?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melinda Ross" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melinda Ross" href="/user/show/1318303-melinda-ross">Melinda Ross</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440411567" href="/book/show/1206171.Calico_Captive">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56039977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11860764660940668987">This book is based on a true story of a family living in Connecticut during the French and Indian war. The family is kidnapped by Indians who had sided with the French. They are forced to march north and then are at periodic times turned over to the French. The family is scattered and must work hard to try to find their way back to each other. The original book was written by the older sister--Susanna years after the ordeal (and was actually made into a made for TV movie years ago). Speare takes</span>
  <span id="freeText11860764660940668987" style="display:none">This book is based on a true story of a family living in Connecticut during the French and Indian war. The family is kidnapped by Indians who had sided with the French. They are forced to march north and then are at periodic times turned over to the French. The family is scattered and must work hard to try to find their way back to each other. The original book was written by the older sister--Susanna years after the ordeal (and was actually made into a made for TV movie years ago). Speare takes the story and tells it from the younger sister's point of view. Though this is the only book Speare was not recognized by the Newberry people for, it is still a wonderful read. I love Miriam's courage not only in the face of the Indians but also her gumption when faced with the proper French. I am always on the look out for books with strong female characters and this one definitely fits the bill.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11860764660940668987" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Along with her family, Miriam is taken away from home and traded from the Indian society into an elite French community.   <span id="freeText3014632662786224277" style="display:none">This is one of my favorite novels from my elementary school days when I was infatuated with Native Americans and their customs. The novel is based upon the story of Susanna Johnson (although the story is centered around her younger sister, Miriam) a captive of the Abenakis Indians. Along with her family, Miriam is taken away from home and traded from the Indian society into an elite French community. I picked this one up again because I recall how taken by it I was when I first read it; though it was not as powerfully effective as it was at age 10, I found it still to be an entertaining read. It is certainly not just recommendable for fifth graders.</span>

This story was based in the time of the French and Indian war beginning in America when a family is kidnapped by Indians and taken from their home in New England to Canada. I have read other books by this author and would willingly read others.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780395071120" href="/book/show/1206172.Calico_Captive">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22241323-judy?shelf=us-vrt-nh-maine-f-nf">us-vrt-nh-maine-f-nf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22241323-judy?shelf=k-fic-historical">k-fic-historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1710719178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11788213865859023453">I grew up spending part of each summer in the mountains where I would pretend I was an 'Indian,' meaning that I would try to walk without leaving sign, learn to recognize plants that were edible, listen to what the birds were 'saying,' learn by watching animal behaviors ... all of which was good for developing observational skills.<br/><br/>Thus, stories about Indian captives always intrigued me. This one was especially appealing since it is based on an actual abduction.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

For the life of me I could not understand why Miriam didn't stay with Pierre since she didn't really spend that much time with Phineas Whitney, but who am I to judge.</span>

<div id="review_2313320453" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2313320453" />
    <a title="Kris Dersch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/77033786-kris-dersch"><img alt="Kris Dersch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517100823p2/77033786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2313320453?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kris Dersch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kris Dersch" href="/user/show/77033786-kris-dersch">Kris Dersch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77033786-kris-dersch?shelf=based-on-true-story">based-on-true-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77033786-kris-dersch?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77033786-kris-dersch?shelf=kidslit">kidslit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/77033786-kris-dersch?shelf=a-family-thing">a-family-thing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2313320453" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4759433722691961141">I was honestly surprised how much I liked this one. I picked it up because I am related to the two women on whose life experience it is based and thought I should at least give it a try, especially since The Witch of Blackbird Pond is so great. But I was definitely expecting it to be...problematic.<br/>First of all, it does require some context. The portrayal of the American Indians is problematic, which I think you'd expect from a 1950s book. But while far from issue-free, I think it is a better por</span>
  <span id="freeText4759433722691961141" style="display:none">I was honestly surprised how much I liked this one. I picked it up because I am related to the two women on whose life experience it is based and thought I should at least give it a try, especially since The Witch of Blackbird Pond is so great. But I was definitely expecting it to be...problematic.<br />First of all, it does require some context. The portrayal of the American Indians is problematic, which I think you'd expect from a 1950s book. But while far from issue-free, I think it is a better portrayal than a lot of books of the era, and what I didn't realize until I started reading is the vast majority of the book is not about the girls being captives of American Indians at all, it's about them being captives of the French in Montreal. This is, of course, the French and Indian war. <br />The English-French animosities of the 18th century is something I'd not thought much about, just sort of knew existed, and of course we are in the hands of an historical fiction master here, so the detail is wonderful and I felt drawn in to 18th century Montreal. Miriam is a relatable and her journey is interesting, even if some of the other characters seem a little like caricatures. I found myself wanting more as the ending leaves us intentionally far from the end of Miriam's story, but an epilogue fills in the gaps for us. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4759433722691961141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Qgxw8FW3FglCYa3wwVt6Xesw0MpMiuPuZbeqoziGCOK0qopOu5ayZjOvK9amfuI39btfy05dyvigYle5Wn2yvg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JuBepFCr8LfvtVbKW8GtHHlRjKsavzXFoLIe9jzF/6bQRqQavopU2J570Ow85DV2Z9oDqhhoHNNlZ+PsXj5F+g==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6142407590125234427">I was born in Melrose, Massachusetts, on November 21, 1908. I have lived all my life in New England, and though I love to travel I can't imagine ever calling any other place on earth home. Since I can't remember a time when I didn't intend to write, it is hard to explain why I took so long getting around to it in earnest. But the years seemed to go by very quickly. In 1936 I married Alden Speare a</span>
  <span id="freeText6142407590125234427" style="display:none">I was born in Melrose, Massachusetts, on November 21, 1908. I have lived all my life in New England, and though I love to travel I can't imagine ever calling any other place on earth home. Since I can't remember a time when I didn't intend to write, it is hard to explain why I took so long getting around to it in earnest. But the years seemed to go by very quickly. In 1936 I married Alden Speare and came to Connecticut. Not till both children were in junior high did I find time at last to sit down quietly with a pencil and paper. I turned naturally to the things which had filled my days and thoughts and began to write magazine articles about family living. Then one day I stumbled on a true story from New England history with a character who seemed to me an ideal heroine. Though I had my first historical novel almost by accident it soon proved to be an absorbing hobby." <br /><br />Elizabeth George Speare (1908-1994) won the 1959 Newbery Medal for THE WITCH OF BLACKBIRD POND, and the 1962 Newbery Medal for THE BRONZE BOW. She also received a Newbery Honor Award in 1983, and in 1989 she was presented with the Laura Ingalls Wilder Award for her substantial and enduring contribution to children’s literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6142407590125234427" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
